And if the corrupt bone is small or removable, then you can remove it with fine forceps that are suitable for this purpose.
But if there are multiple bones, then carefully extract all of them with your strength and skill, leaving behind no cause for concern.
But if any of the humors present a difficulty or danger during incision, then carefully cut through the ligaments or flesh surrounding them and release the bones from their grip.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then cover the wound with a dressing that will keep it clean and allow it to heal around the bones. Keep the wound open but protected while waiting for a later opportunity to remove the bones and ensure that the incision does not become infected or close before then.

Concerning the care of sinuses and the quality of an enema with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy limbs and the space of the abscess is prolonged so that it collects pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and pus is excreted from it and the area remains empty like a vessel, and the skin that is above it is as if thinned out like a thin cloth, and corruption has not reached behind it, and does not penetrate into bone or nerves or ligaments, it is called a sinus and is not named a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged to the point where corruption reaches a member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment for it is that you insert all of the skin as it has rolled up, and especially when it has been thinned out and made like cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the location due to its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not reached behind it due to a subsequent consequence, and there is an opening in the skin, then you cure this with an enema.

On the removal of arrows.
Arrows are diverse in kind according to their material and the places in which they become embedded, for their diversity consists of those with large heads, small ones, hollow or blunt, solid or dull, with three sharp edges, or those with tongues, or those with feathered shafts (astelas).
And the signs of imminent death in the brain are when an arrow strikes it and passes through the skull, which is above the brain, causing violent headache, blind spots or loss of vision, dizziness, tingling or numbness in the extremities, inflammation and burning in the tongue, seizures, confusion, vomiting of bile, sudden loss of speech, and swelling or discharge from the wound site that resembles fine white powder or watery flesh.
And therefore if you see these signs, then it is necessary to refrain from treating and removing the arrow, unless absolutely necessary.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart when it is close to the left breast, and you feel as if it has been driven into a hard surface and not penetrated deeply, and the gentle movement of the arrow resembles that of the pulse, and there is black blood flowing from the wound, and the extremities become cold and clammy, and there is fainting. Therefore, know that death is imminent.
And the signs when an arrow has pierced the lung are: the discharge of foamy blood from the wound; inflammation and swelling in the veins near the neck; the patient's complexion becomes pale or blue; there is a loud, wheezing sound upon exhalation; and they gasp for breath.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane that is in the foot, and the pain is near the small ribs, then there is severe pain and breathing difficulties, but not all members or muscles are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then there will quickly be discharge of undigested food from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the belly and becomes embedded in it, then there will be a tear or rupture of the intestine or membrane, and there is little hope for the cure of the patient by extracting the arrow.
And if the arrowhead lodges in the bladder, and urine comes out of it, and some cause makes it come out from the outside, and a strong pain is present on the patient, then you should know that it is mortal.
But other parts of the body, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders and collarbones, spine, tailbone, hip, and leg, are spared according to the greatest dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not venomous.

On venesection (phlebotomy).
The veins that are customarily used for venesection are usually only three. Of those, sixteen are located in the head. There are two pulsatile veins near the ears, which are called "auricularis"; two herbal veins on the temples; two lacrimal veins in the inner corner of each eye, also known as "aspicientiae"; a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the tip of the nose. There are two jugular veins in the neck; two veins in the upper lip; two veins in the lower lip; and these last ones are called "angularis." Additionally, there are two veins under the tongue. But only five veins are customarily bled from the arms. For of these five, one is the cephalic vein, which is located near the wild artery, and is called "head vein" by the people; it is a medium-sized vein, composed of a branch of the basilica and another of the cephalic. This one is known as the "whole body vein," and the basilic vein is the one located in the domestic artery, called "citillar." The common people call it "belly vein"; funis del bras, which is located above the wrist bone and is bled from. It is apparent on the pulse for all to see; salvatella, which is between the auricular and little finger, consisting of two branches.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins: one of which is located below the curvature of the ankle near the wild artery; the second is saphenous, and its location is below the calf muscle of the domestic artery; and sciatic vein, whose location is below the calf muscle near the wild artery. In the leg there are other similar veins to these.
The use of the veins near the ears is for ancient and migraine headaches, as well as scabies and old ulcers of the head.
And the method for bleeding those veins is as follows: make an incision on the patient's head; wet the patient's head with water at the occiput where both veins are located; apply a wide cloth with good friction to this area. Then strengthen the patient's neck with a band so that both ears and the locations of their veins appear in two depressed areas on the head. And then seek out those veins with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those with your finger, then you have found them.
And then you take the cutting scalpel, which is sharp, and insert it under the vein at the wrist until the venesection reaches the bone. And then lift your hand with the vein and wrist toward the area above, and make an incision into the vein with a sharp cut. And let the length of the incision be the length of two fingers adjusted or close to that, and allow blood to flow out as much as you want.
And then strengthen with bandages and leave it in place until it is healed.
And I have already mentioned at the beginning of this book the incision and its cauterization.
But the benefit of venesection from the two temples arteries is for ancient and persistent headaches, chronic eye problems, and acute inflammation in the limbs.
And the method for bleeding those veins is as follows: constrict the patient's neck so that both veins appear clearly and their pulses are apparent under your finger, and then you have found them.

On cupping and the qualities of using that method.
Cupping is used for corns, or for boils, or for abscesses.
Cupping is done in two ways: one is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is also done in two ways: one is with fire, and the other is without fire.
And the cups that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are applied to 14 locations on the body, of which one is where the cups are placed, and this is at the nape of the neck, and the middle location on it from both sides of the neck together; and cups under the chin below the jaw more below the mouth; and cups of two spoons; and cups of spleen, and this is above the end of the coccyx; and cups of two eyes, and they are two inches wide; and cups of two knees; and cups of two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood from the small veins spread in the flesh, and for that reason it does not lose its virtue through those, as is clearly seen in phlebotomy; nor is it necessary that cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body has been evacuated.
But if there is a need for cupping due to illness or chronic pain, then administer it at any time, both at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of the illness and at some intervals.
And therefore when you see any of those causes, and particularly if it is in the third or middle of the month, then we command that cupping be done more than two or three hours after sunrise.
And the benefit of cupping at the nape is that when it alleviates heaviness in the head and what is sunken in the eyes; but it is necessary that this be followed by evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the location of bloodletting on the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use cupping on those who have a fever or a cold, as it can cause great harm.
And therefore it is not appropriate to use cupping on the elderly or those with frail health and chronic illnesses.
But for those who have continual sweating after cupping, it is appropriate to instruct the cupping practitioner to pause their hand a little before the cup at the lower parts, in order to prevent forgetfulness (oblivion) from being generated.
But cupping at the nape is done in the location of bloodletting on the fusca and basilica veins: it alleviates asthma, constriction of the windpipe, pain in the chest, cough, and repletion (of humors).
And it is appropriate to apply the cupping cup at the nape slightly, because if it is placed too low, it can cause weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping at the temples alleviates accidental pains in the head, ophthalmia, migraines, and pre-fainting, as well as toothaches, and it is done in the location of bloodletting on the basilic vein.
And it is necessary to instruct the bleeder not to press his hand on the incision, so as not to injure the artery and cause excessive bleeding.
And apply cupping glasses below the chin with alcohol in the mouth, and to rotten gums, and similar to other ailments that are in the mouth, and in place of fleabites on the two lips.
But the cupping glass that is made on top of two spatulas refers to a cupping horn, which is done by infusion of heat.
And the cupping glass in the two parts of the temples refers to help from the fleam-bite of three veins, that is, the basilic, fusca and cephalica, for this draws blood from all those veins that are deeper in the flesh, and those smaller veins from other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
Instruct the bleeder not to proceed too much with incisions, for the place is stripped of flesh and nerves and arteries are underneath.
And this is a cupping glass with which one cups the hips and it helps with hemorrhoids of the buttocks and ulcers that are lower down.
And it is necessary to instruct the cupping practitioner that it should be a large cup, and made of iron, for the place requires strong incision; and light, subtle cups should be trepanned and incised with incisions.
But smaller cups are for minor, clear manifestations, for they draw blood from all the body, and help with old pains in the loins and uterus, and bladder; and they provoke menstruation, and help with flatulence and colic; and they are in place of fleabites on two temples, and below the collarbones, and on two knees.
However, they weaken the whole body and cause fainting in some men.
And the help of cupping glasses below the collarbones is similar to the help of cups on two temples.
And the quality of the position of cups is that you place the cup in the beginning empty and draw it out gradually, and do not prolong the position of cups, but rather place them lightly, and leave them to cool down, and let the humors come to the place through natural arrival. And do not stop repeating this and continuing until you see that the place is red and swollen and the reddening of blood appears.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and at this point carefully consider the condition of the body.
For a person with tender flesh and little fat, it is necessary to incise them once and not more, so that the place does not become ulcerated.
Instruct the cupping practitioner to widen the incision and make it a little deeper, and moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary to incise twice, for in the first incision the passage of the subtlety of blood and its moisture is opened. But in the second one, seek the careful extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very viscous, then make a third incision in order to reach the last of it.
And finally, when we want to extract blood little by little or a small amount, one incision is sufficient for us.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, make many incisions; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make deep incisions.
And the limit of incision, which is only as deep as the skin.

And a cup is placed on the loins when there is accumulation or swelling, for by its strong attraction it removes accumulation and eliminates swelling from its place. [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loin#Medical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loin#Medical)
Similarly, it does when a cup is placed on the liver and spleen to remove windiness trapped inside them. [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spleen#Medical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spleen#Medical)
And these cups are administered either only with suction, or sometimes with heat.
And sometimes cups are administered full of thick water in illnesses of the spleen. (It is not entirely clear what "thick water" refers to here, but it may mean a warm or medicinal solution)
And this is done so that the cup is full and becomes larger, that is, with warm water alone or with a solution in which appropriate herbs have been cooked. Form of cup administered with heat: the width of its opening should be two fingers, as we have made it; and the quantity of air inside should be the size of a palm; and in the place of it, in the area that is within the circuit, the size of it, the hole should be small according to the amount that fits into it a needle. It should be made of iron or citrus wood, with wide edges, smooth and even, so that it does not harm the member in its position on it. And cupping glasses on infants and thin people are weaker than cupping glasses on adults and robust bodies.
And the way to apply this cupping glass with fire to a member is to extend the wick with a decent fire of wood before a small candle of wax, and place it in the middle of the transverse vein in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the area below, to prevent the patient's flesh from being burned.

But in the case of a cupping glass administered for pleurisy, with water, which has no transverse vein or spot, and it is not applied with fire but instead filled with water, and placed on the member only.[English Translation by me]
And this is the method for it: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, so that it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Capital Chapter XCVII. (97)
Leeches are not administered on many members but only on those members where there is no possibility of placing cupping glasses due to their small size, such as the mouth, gums and similar areas; or on a bare member without flesh, such as the finger and nose and similar areas.
And the way to use those that have been found, leeches, which are in clean muddy waters.
And then they should be left in dirty water during the day and night until they are hungry, and there is no food left in their stomachs; and then the body should be evacuated at the beginning with bloodletting and cupping glasses; and then the affected member should be rubbed until it turns red; and then they can be placed on the patient. [Note: The leeches are starved for few days before using them, so that they will suck the blood effectively, also bleeding is used to cleanse the body and preparing the area where leeches will be placed.]
And when they are full and satisfied. (Note: When the leeches have finished sucking the blood, they will fall off by themselves)
And if it is possible to suck from the spot with a cupping glass, it is more effective in treatment. [Note: The use of cupping glasses over the leech may help in extracting more blood and also ensure that the leech falls off after it's done sucking.]
And if not, then wash the spot with sour wine and then rinse it with clean water; and then rub it and squeeze (the area)
And if the bleeding from the leeches is prolonged, and there is oozing of blood, then soak a piece of linen in cold water and place it on top until the oozing stops. [Note: This step is done to stop any further bleeding by applying pressure with a cold wet cloth]
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle vitriol triscad on it, or other styptic agents, until the bleeding stops; or place crushed dry broad beans over the spot and leave them on until they stick to the skin, and the bleeding will stop. [Note: Vitriol Triscad is a styptic agent that helps in stopping bleeding, similarly dry beans when applied on the wound will also help in stopping bleeding by sticking to the skin, this method is still used today in some cultures.]
And it is necessary, if required, to repeat the application of leeches, when it is possible to change them (with new ones)
And if the use of leeches is forbidden or not possible, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the spots with a needle until there is bleeding; and then place them (leeches) because they will detach as soon as they feel any blood. [Note: This step is done to ensure that the leech attaches to the skin and starts sucking blood, sometimes due to various reasons leeches may not attach to the skin so alternative methods are used.]
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little bit of aloe triscad powder, or salt, or ash on them, and they will detach immediately. [Note: This step is done to ensure that the leech falls off after it's done sucking blood, this can be done by using any of the mentioned substances which are irritant in nature and will make the leech detach from the skin.]
Thus begins the topic of the third section: on restoration. (Note: The text is divided into three sections, the first one is about bloodletting and cupping glasses, the second one is about leeches, and the third one is about restoration or healing) This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine, and deals with the restoration of fractures and dislocations caused by accidents in bones.
Oh son, know that foolish doctors and the uneducated boast of this chapter, those who have never looked at the books of the ancients or read a single letter of them. And for this reason, it has come to be that this knowledge is lost in our land; and I have never forgotten it at any time; and I have not used anything new, but only through the long study of ancient texts and understanding of them, until I extracted the science from those. (Note: The author states that he has gained his medical knowledge through extensive reading and studying of ancient texts, rather than relying on unproven methods)
And I also directed myself to practice and study throughout my entire life. (Note: The author emphasizes the importance of continuous learning, practice, and self-improvement in medicine)
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science contains and on which I have conducted experiments; since here I have summarized it for you and delivered it from the burden of long explanations, and I have summarized it in summary form; and I have demonstrated it in the final demonstration; and I have formed many forms of instruments used in this, as is done by adding explanation, as I have done in two previous chapters.
Capital First.
Summary and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from which a preliminary mention is necessary before we begin to remember the members (bones) that are broken and the different types of each one. (Note: The author will give an overview of different types of fractures and bones.) Therefore, it is necessary that we say in the preamble of this chapter the summaries of the discourse, and the chapters whose understanding is required at the beginning for the artist (doctor) to focus on the truth of them, so that you and those who are ready to learn or understand this noble art from you.
Therefore, I say that when a fracture or dislocation or sprain or swelling occurs, it is necessary to begin with bloodletting or purging of the affected area, or both if there is no prohibition such as debility of virtue, or it is an infant, or elderly in decrepitude, or during times of intense heat or cold.
And then the patient should be satisfied with a diet of fresh herbs and bird meat and crab; and we prohibit heavy meats and filling up on food and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain there is no danger of abscess, and if there is no fear of effusion of matter to the member.
And then return the patient to their usual regimen, according to their customary lifestyle. (Note: The author suggests that after treating injuries and fractures, the patient should return to their normal routine as soon as possible, to help them recover faster and better)
Therefore, when the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary that the patient be fed nutritious foods that are thick and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as food made from wheat cooked with water, rice, peas, beef, mutton, fresh fish, red wine and similar things. And with this diet, the coagulation of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
Know that bones that are broken in men who are in the prime of youth and in the elderly, cannot be continuous and consolidated according to their nature due to the dryness of their members. (Note: The author states that fractured bones in young people and elderly may not heal properly due to dryness of their body)
However, nature adapts and nourishes the broken bone with some cause similar to glue that has thickness which attaches and strengthens it until they are consolidated and united together, until they reach the end of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits it from its actions.
And for this reason, it is necessary to stop the patient's food, foods that are heavy and viscous and thick, as we have said. (Note: The author states that in order for the bone to heal properly, it is important to avoid foods that are heavy and thick, which can interfere with the natural healing process)
Know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of bones, for a fracture in the bone of the leg is different from a fracture in the bone of the head; and a fracture in the bone of the thigh is different from a fracture in the bone of the back; and similarly all other bones are different from each other.
And I will remember all the types of fractures, explained in their own separate chapter. (Note: The author states that he will give a detailed explanation of each type of fracture in its own chapter)
Many varieties of fractures within themselves (Note: This statement indicates that the author will discuss different types of fractures depending on their characteristics) And the types of bone fractures are diverse in their manifestations; for there exist bent fractures, others that result in splinters; and certain fractures occur along the length of the bone: and such are the fractures of stability and instability, as well as those with or without additional pieces, just like in writing preparation, and this is a fracture with contusion and dislocation at the head, and this is a small fissure fracture.
And each type brings its own specific pain and requires individualized treatment based on the distinct memory that comes with it in its respective location.
Signs of a bone fracture. And so a bone fracture is recognized by its deformity and the prominence or appearance of it to touch, as well as through compression with one's hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent deformity or if none is present, and you do not feel any movement when touching the bone with agitation, and the patient does not experience significant pain, then it is not a fracture.
It is possible that there may be a deformity or small fissure, in which case you should not manipulate it with your hand or apply excessive compression; instead, place the appropriate medication on it, based on what comes to mind at that moment for that location, and use a fine, gentle bandage to hold it in place.
And know that when a location is bent and not split in two or otherwise dislocated but rather unstable, like the entire part of its comparison, then you should begin immediately with correcting it by applying a cautery for abscesses.
And if an abscess forms after cautery, then leave it alone for several days until the abscess subsides; and then dress it as lightly and comfortably as possible in any way you can.
And know that restoring and aligning a fractured bone is easier than healing a broken bone with stability issues.
And strengthen it according to what comes to mind about its specific needs.
But if the bone is unstable, then it may be necessary to extend or realign the broken limb in two parts, whether it's an arm or a leg; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes or with one rope and your hand.
And your position when placing the limb in its proper location should be according to its natural shape, while extending the broken parts of the limb; then, you must ensure that those additional pieces are returned with care and in their proper places as much as possible.
And be mindful of your strength to avoid causing pain or injury to the patient during your operation; and strive, within your ability, to align the broken bone pieces together in their best possible positions.
And at that time, you should touch the affected area with your hand and, if you notice any other cause for concern, correct it and adjust it according to the degree of your skill or expertise.
And avoid excessive extension and compression, just as foolish doctors often do, who cause abscesses from their operations or cause muscle contractions in the limb, as we have seen happen many times before with such careless practitioners.
And the quality of the bandage for the immobilized limb should be as recommended, because you should know that fractured limbs are diverse in their smallness and largeness, and in the shape of each one.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and finger and similar ones, then the wrapping should be of very fine fabric.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, then a broad and hard wrapping is required, because heavy bandages connect the large and strong member in all parts with firm pressure, so that it does not become infected.
And begin with attaching, adjusting the alignment, and pausing appropriately for that, over the site of the fracture with three or four wrappings, according to the size of the limb, and strengthen your hand a little with the bandage.
And then move to the upper part of the site of the fracture, and strengthen less than you had tightened the bandage at the site of the fracture. And then gradually extend the bandage away from the site of the fracture, and loosen the pressure slightly, until you have covered a large portion of the area.
And then take another bandage and wrap it around the other limb over the broken area with bandages, and move the bandages down to below the fracture; and your operation should be in the tightness of the bandage and the loosening of that as we have said in the first bandaging above.
And then place between the layers of wool or fabric that is evenly woven, to correct the deformation of the fracture if there is any deformity in it; and if not, do not stop for any reason.
And then place it on another bandage, and then align these layers evenly made so that there is no inflammation or abscess in the area; and if not, then place it on top of the abscess that has formed and remove any inflammation; and leave it for several days; and then strengthen the layers.
And the layers should be made of hard cane or similar material, and they should be well-formed; or made from wood or palm branches; and the width of each layer should correspond to this shape and form.
However, it is necessary that the layer placed over the fracture be slightly larger and wider than the other layers, and the length of the layer should correspond to the size of the limb in magnitude and smallness; and then strengthen the layers with another bandage according to your first tightening, and then tie it on top with a string as we have said about tension, which is that your tightening should be according to the location of the fracture more, and the further away from the fracture, the less tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and subtlety, and they should be of fine linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen with some foolish practitioners using cords of twisted hair, this is a great error, because it disrupts the equal tightness.
And also thin threads are not suitable, because you will not be able to achieve the desired tightness with those, and it is not appropriate for there to be less than a finger width between layer and layer.
And if the patient is pricked by the ends of the needles causing constriction in his limbs, then place a piece of sponge or crimson wool under him so that it does not cause injury.
Some fractures are like a wound and occur when there is a rupture in the skin, and this will be addressed in the second chapter under the category of singularity.
And know that not all broken limbs should be treated with needles on the first day; this is because when the limb is large, it is not advisable to apply needles immediately but rather wait for 2-7 days or more, depending on your confidence in avoiding cautery-induced abscess.
And this is the method of making poultices that the ancients used to treat fractures, dislocations, and twists.
Here is the method of making a poultice suitable for healing fractures, appropriate for many complexities and particularly for women and children because it does not generate heat or friction: take some flour powder from the mill, which is the medulla of the flour produced by the movement of the millstone against the wall of the mill.
And do the same with this one as before, but instead of using plain water, use egg white and make a thicker and more substantial composition, and then apply it.
Another way to make an ointment that heals fractures and dislocations and twists: take one drachma each of asafetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and migrach (which is wild marjoram), mix them together; add five drachmas each of myrrh and aloe; then mix twenty drachms of aloe wood; sift and grind all the ingredients and mix them with almond oil or egg white if the patient's complexion is dry; then apply this ointment, which has a good composition and restores broken bones lightly, and benefits many people.
Another way to make an ointment that helps with fractures and twists: take one drachma each of marjoram, white mallow, myrrh, aloe, acacia, and twenty drachmas of Armenian bole.
Grind all the ingredients together in a mortar with moderate pressure, then sift them and mix them with water or egg white before administering it as a poultice to the affected area.
Here is a method for an emplaster suitable for joints and bones that have become separated from their proper places, as well as for pain and swelling in those areas and for fissures and fractures: take soft wool and soak it in vinegar and oil of quince, then apply it to the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of healing or causing cauterization abscesses, but it does drive out pain specifically.
Here is another method for a plaster that can heal broken bones: take leaves from the wild poppy tree and fig trees, grind them together, and make a poultice from their juices.
Here is a method for a simplified poultice that promotes healing of bone fractures and helps resolve residual abscess: take equal parts of mallow root, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, barley flour, all mixed together in equal parts. Grind all the ingredients into a powder and mix them with vinegar, as long as the limb is not injured or inflamed.
However, if it is inflamed, then do the same with this one but instead of vinegar, use water infused with hollyhock or plain water and apply it. Hollyhock water has anti-inflammatory properties that can help soothe irritation and promote healing.
Here is another method of a stronger plaster for resolving abscesses that occurs during bone fracture healing: take equal parts of mallow root, linseed, balmony, sweet clover, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile, all ground to a powder; mix them with the water of salvian or plain water or vinegar, according to the nature of the limb and its complexion. This plaster is stronger than the previous one and should be used when there is an abscess that is hard during the healing process.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that a plaster should be placed on the broken limb for its healing but rather recommended using a cerate made from wax and oil, with no other ingredients; he advised that it should be of medium consistency between being too thick and too thin.
However, Galen recommends that on the broken limb for its healing, plasters made of drying substances with mild heat should be applied, such as myrrh, aloes and similar substances to those previously mentioned.
Here is a method for a plaster that can heal wounds and relieve pain: take equal parts of myrtle leaves, human hair or bird feathers, mallow root, and salt; grind them together, sift them, and make a poultice from the resulting powder.
However, the amount of water used to make the poultice should be sufficient to keep it moist and pliable, but not so much that it becomes runny or loses its effectiveness. If there is no pain, swelling, or displacement of bones, then the poultice can be left on for several days. And if pain or swelling or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should reconsider or remove the poultice immediately and apply a new one that is more appropriate for the symptoms present.
And take a piece of linen and a wet sea sponge, and soak them in warm water, and use those to gently wash the area until the swelling goes down and the pain is relieved; then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place over it soft wool soaked in vinegar, oil or rose oil, and bind it with bandages for one night until you are sure there will be no abscess and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided and the pain is gone.
And then gradually reduce the constriction by applying a light poultice that is not too tight, and loosen it as healing progresses.
And if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation and all symptoms have been completely healed, then apply the poultice and constriction as before.
And if none of the symptoms mentioned earlier occur in the limb, then do not remove the poultice for three to four or five to seven days. And sometimes the poultice is left on for 20 days, depending on your judgment based on the swelling of the limb, as mentioned earlier, until the fracture heals and the flesh forms a scab over it.
Then adjust the constriction so that it is tighter than the original constriction and match it to the swelling of the patient's food intake, as previously indicated in their memory.
But if you see that the site of the fracture is drying and becoming more atrophied than necessary, then you should know that nutrition is not reaching it; therefore, moisten it with warm water every time you change the poultice for three days, and slightly loosen the constriction to allow nutrition to reach the limb and promote gentle healing.
But what foolish restorers of fractured limbs often do when the fracture is not healed at the beginning, according to their judgment, and it heals crookedly, is a mistake according to their method, and they should be greatly afraid; if they were right in doing so, the ancients would have praised them for their work.
And I have not seen any of them do this, and therefore it is more reasonable not to do so.
Chapter two: On Fracture in the Head
Chapter two: On Fracture of the Head
The severity of these two types of fractures can be great or small, and there is another type of fracture called contusio or attrition. It is caused by percussion with a blunt object on stone or similar surfaces.
This type of fracture can be penetrating, which means it approaches the panniculus that is below the bone surface, or it can occur on the face of the bone. The severity of these two types of fractures can also be great or small.
There is another type of fracture that occurs in the subtlety of the hairline and is therefore called "capillary fracture".
This type of fracture is caused by a blow or impact with a stone or similar object, which causes a piece of the bone to be forced inward into the inner parts. It occurs in concave areas, similar to how the iron rods of a plow can break and penetrate inward when struck. This type of fracture is called "laminar fracture".
And this type of fracture is most commonly seen in the bones of children and infants, whose skulls are not yet fully formed and remain soft and malleable.
And all these types of fractures are dangerous, just like separated needles. I will now move on to discussing the treatment for each type of skull fracture in its respective place.
In order to diagnose these types of fractures, we must perform detection and examination of the wound, as well as removal of any corrupt flesh that may be present on top of it. This is because the appearance of the wound can provide clues about the type and extent of the underlying fracture. For example, a depressed fracture (ascular) may have a sunken area or indentation, while a linear fracture (levatio absoluta) may show a clear break in the skin. Similarly, a comminuted fracture (fractura capillar) may present as multiple small fragments, while an open fracture (fractura penetrant) may have bone protruding through the wound. Therefore, careful examination of the wound and surrounding area is essential for accurate diagnosis and appropriate treatment of skull fractures.
The capillary fracture can be identified by the appearance of a black line on the surface of the bone after it has been scraped clean and dried with a cloth. This black line indicates the presence of a hairline crack or fissure in the bone, which may not be visible to the naked eye but can still cause symptoms such as pain, swelling, and tenderness.
When treating a fracture, it is important to consider the symptoms of the patient at the beginning of treatment, and then if you see any signs that indicate a serious condition, such as vomiting from colic or extreme pain, loss of consciousness, loss of speech, fainting, high fever, swelling or redness of the limbs, or similar symptoms in severe cases, you should suspect that the fracture is not healing properly.
If you do not see any unusual symptoms and there are no signs of complications, then you can begin the treatment for the fracture.
The reason for this is that if the patient comes to you in the beginning of the illness while it is still winter, then you must study the state of the bone before the 14th day, regardless of any other factors. This is because during the winter months, bones can take longer to heal due to the colder temperatures and reduced blood flow. Therefore, it is essential to monitor the progress of the fracture closely during this time and adjust the treatment plan accordingly.
And if it is in the summer days, it is necessary to make sure that the wound in front of the bone is healed within the eleventh day, having made sure that what is beneath the skin has been corrupted by pus, and that the complications that we have mentioned have occurred.
And if the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and it is in small fragments and crushed pieces, then it is necessary to saw through the comminuted and crushed parts, as I will instruct you.
And you must raise the patient's head and uncover the bone as is customary, insofar as it is possible for you according to the shape of the wound, and make it light on the And if you encounter bone detachment, flow of blood or abscess, then you should oppose it with what is necessary, that is, apply dampened cloths with wine and oil to the area, and make sure the wound rests until the swelling subsides and you are certain that there is no risk of blood flowing; then begin the circulation and removal of it.
And this should be done in one of two ways of operation.
And one way is that you insert the bone with straight-edged scissors, and this is its form: Then administer another scissor, slightly wider than the first one; and this is its form.
And then administer larger scissors, wider than the second one.
And it is necessary that you have several pairs of scissors with different widths, some wider than others; and some of them should be shorter than the others and sharp at the ends, made of iron or steel.
And use them easily in percussion on the scissors so that you do not damage the head and harm it.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary that you pierce its circumference before using scissors with trocars, which are called non-penetrating trocars.
And these are not called that because they do not reach the end of the bone up to its head, but rather because their round extremity or edge has a shape similar to a small circle or a sharp cap, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate that you take a large number of these trocars, each one suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have the sharp extremity of the skull present in all tests of the brain, with its length and width corresponding to the thickness of this bone.
And this is the form of three types of trocars: The quality of piercing the circumference of a broken bone is that you place the trocar on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then remove the trocar to another location, and place what is between all the holes the thickness of the ray or approximately that.
And then use scissors to cut what is between each hole of the bone, making it as thin as possible for lightness, as we have said, until you extract the bone with your hand or due to some other cause using certain instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, such as tongs and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you exercise great caution so that the scissors do not touch any part of the brain.
And once you have pierced the bone and separated it from the brain if it is attached to it, and have extracted it, then it is necessary to scrape and sharpen the roughness of it using a tool similar to scissors but rather thinner and sharper than the other scissors.
And if there remains any small and stubborn part of the bones, then take it easily with what you have available; then care for the wound with liniments and ointments that we have mentioned.
And the other way of operating is light and distant from danger, which is called Galen's method and is highly praised; and this is its meaning: it is necessary for you at the beginning to reveal the part of the bone where the fracture is most intense and clear, until when you have revealed that part and placed the extremity of this scissor gently and slowly, which has this form.
And it is that the lenticular part is lion-shaped and does not penetrate any cause, and the sharp part is extended from it in two parts, advancing in length as you see; so that the lenticular part is close to the brain with its sharp face cutting the bone.
And then strike the scissors with a small hammer until you penetrate the entire bone easily and as it rotates, making sure that no fat enters it in any way, and if the patient is the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them, certainly, and even if they are sleeping.
And if there remains any attached cause to the bone of the fat in certain parts of it, then you will cut it with the lenticular extremity of the scissors and deliver it easily, for it is removed without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the fat, but rather the surface or a piece of the bone remains rough and has subtle stubborn parts, then you must scrape that roughness and extract those stubborn parts with fine razors that you have prepared in various quantities so that you can use them conveniently in all locations that require it, and straighten what is necessary according to your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that you use the razor with which you scrape the bone at the beginning, the widest of them; and then gradually move to the finest one, and do not stop doing so until you reach using the finest and sharpest of all of them.
But in other minor capillary and subtle fractures, it is necessary to provide care according to what you find necessary for the correction of them.
And it is the case that not all those who have studied this art agree on it. And this relates to what we have written about large fractures.
And if the fat protrudes when you extract the bone, then it is necessary to take a cloth of appropriate size for the wound, soak it in oiled wine, and place it on the opening of the wound; then take another folded or tripled cloth, soak it in vinegar and oiled wine, and place it over the first cloth lightly, without pressing it hard against the brain, but rather gently and only as much as is necessary to cover the wound.
And leave it with this care for a day or two until you are certain that there is no abscess; then remove the cloth and administer medicines that are necessary to treat excess dryness, such as linseed and barley flour, and powder of lentils and hartshorn, and similar things.
And it is that you make powder of these medicines and sprinkle it over the wound while they are dry; and universally, administer all medicine that has the property of cleansing and healing without causing harm or biting.
And take care to ensure that the wound is clean during your care; and let it not become contaminated with pus or any other impurities, nor allow virulence to accumulate in it; because when it is close to the brain's fat, it will corrupt it and weaken it, causing great harm to the patient.
And if there is a blackening on the surface of the skull, it may indicate that the bone has become corrupt and especially if care for it has been neglected; therefore, consider carefully whether the patient experiences any of the symptoms we have mentioned, as this indicates impending danger.
And if the blackening is not due to the medicine applied but rather from its properties, then it is necessary that you take one part of honey and three parts of oiled wine. And soak a cloth with these liquids and place it over the skull; and care for it with appropriate healing spices until it heals.
Chapter III
About restoring the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but rather two higher parts are separated, or one of them is dislocated; therefore, there are two bones in it, as the lower part is made of cartilage and is not broken, but rather it experiences abrasion, twisting, and curvature.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary to insert a small piece of wood into the nostril and apply ointment inside the fracture with your finger; and also outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do so easily, and take care that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the highest part of the nose and it cannot reach it, then it is necessary to make it even with the end of a rod that has a small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then do the same thing.
And try to restore it with a dressing on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approach the wound or ulcer carefully and provide relief for the abscess through cauterization.
And insert a piece of linen thread into the nostril's opening if the fracture is in one part, or place two pads if the fracture is in two parts, and ensure that the padding is large enough to fill the nasal cavity.
As some ancient restorers have said, the pads should be soaked in bread mold, and changed every day, but I do not agree with this; instead, it is better for the padding to be soaked in egg white albumin and powdered chalk; and then leave it long enough for the bone to harden and for the cartilage to strengthen.
And in some cases, insert wicks into the nostrils instead of pads, wrapped in a linen cloth like a lion's tail; and ensure that the break in the nose is not too severe and that the patient is not having difficulty breathing through their nose.
And this measure is not necessary for you; and if you wish to do so, proceed with the wicks, and if you prefer, use pads instead.
And if an abscess or ulcer forms in the nostrils, apply a compress made of wax or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or aloe.
But if an abscess or ulcer does not form, then it is better to apply a poultice made of cumin flour and powdered herbs mixed with egg white albumin; and place a cloth lion's paw over it, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the bones in the nose are broken due to a small fracture or have been crushed, then it is better to cut open the area and extract any debris using appropriate instruments; and then stitch up the incision, and treat the wound with materials that promote healing and binding.
And if a wound forms inside the nose, then it is better to treat it with pads and insert lead tubes that promote complete healing.
Capital Four.
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is fractured.
When the jawbone has been broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then observe whether the fracture is external only, and not split into two parts but curved inward. In this case, the diagnosis is relatively easy.
And so, if the fracture is on the right side of the jawbone, then insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jawbone, then insert your right index finger. Gently push the swelling of the fracture inward towards the center with this finger, while simultaneously using your other hand to gently press from the outside of the jawbone towards the center. Do this evenly and carefully.
And if the jawbone is already split into two parts, then it is necessary to apply extension to both parts in a straight line as much as possible, so that they can be brought back into alignment.
And if there is loosening or dislocation of the teeth, then use wire made of gold, silver, or lead to bind together those that you believe will remain; and place wax over the broken jawbone, followed by a double layer of cloth, and then a thick bandage made of sturdy fabric, or a wooden splint that fits the length of the jawbone. Then attach this bandage or splint securely in place, ensuring that it is properly aligned and will not be destroyed during healing.
And order the patient to rest and remain calm, and eat soft foods that are easy to chew.
And if you believe that the shape of the jawbone has been altered in some way, then observe and release the wire on the third day; and then gently realign and reset any areas that have been distorted or misshapen. Apply a poultice made of powdered herbs, egg white albumin, or cumin flour to the area, using the wax that was previously used as a sealant. Then place wool over the poultice to hold it in place and promote healing.
And once the poultice has been applied and properly aligned with the jawbone, leave it in place until the fracture has healed completely and regained its strength; as this type of fracture often heals within three weeks.
And if a wound or abscess forms in the area, then apply the treatments that we have mentioned several times for healing and reducing inflammation until it has been cured and the wound has healed completely.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then observe whether any fragments of bone are protruding or missing. If this is the case, be gentle and use appropriate instruments to remove any debris or bone fragments from the area during healing.
And if the wound is narrow, then widen it using a small incision or cutting tool, as needed.
And once any fragments have been removed from the wound, then close it with stitches if it is wide enough; or apply a poultice made of appropriate materials to promote healing and encourage tissue growth, until it has fully healed.
Capital Five.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Often, the collarbone is broken in three different ways along its front side near the muscle, and these breaks occur in one of three ways.
Because the break can occur in one of three ways: either it is cleanly broken, or it is separated into two parts without any fragments present, which makes it easier to treat; or there are fragments present, which makes it difficult to treat; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the procedure for treating a fracture that is accompanied by a wound involves having two assistants present. One should hold and support the broken collarbone while the other extends the neck towards the affected area.
Then align the fracture using your fingers until it is properly set in its natural shape, ensuring that there are no bumps or depressions present.
And if more extension is needed, then place a roll of cloth or wool under the patient's neck to provide additional support. Adjust the size of the roll as necessary to ensure that it provides adequate support for the broken collarbone. Use your hand to gently extend and elevate the bone while also compressing the roll to bring the fracture back into alignment.
And if you are unable to pull the ends of the collarbone outward because it has become lodged against the sternum, then have the patient lie on their back and place a medium-sized cushion under their neck.
And have the assistant press down on the muscle near the affected area to lift and elevate the bone that is lodged against the sternum, allowing you to realign and set the fracture with your fingers.
But if you feel fragments of bone in the area that are loose and easily movable, then carefully cut into the affected area to remove these fragments with ease.
And if there are fragments of bone that are deeply embedded in the affected area, then carefully use one of your scalpels to make an incision and remove these fragments with precision. Here is the shape of the instrument you should use:
And it is similar to the shape of a cylinder, with a depression in its center, and its width should be adjusted according to your needs based on the size or smallness of the affected bone.
But its length should be adjusted according to your needs for the operation, and it should have two ends, one wide and one narrow.
And if the wound that you have created when removing bone fragments is wide and there is a risk of infection or abscess, then stitch together the edges of the wound using surgical thread.
And if the wound is small or there is a risk of infection, then apply dressings and poultices to the area based on the size of the incision.
And if a pustule (swelling) occurs due to cauterization, then apply a wet cloth with roasted oil and vinegar on it, and place it on top of it.
And make sure that the splint is not causing harm on the broken bone; and so, it is necessary to see the patient every day, if you see that the swelling has softened or notice that the splint has become too tight, then correct it and strengthen the binding.
Then place the patient's sleep on their side, with a cushion under their ankle and a small cushion under their knee, so that with these supports the patient's body is raised, and the fracture of the collarbone (furcula) is also raised by the elevation of the muscle, or have their arm tied to the neck.
And do not loosen the binding unless pus develops at the site, within 12 days.
Then renew the plaster if you see that the surface of the wound needs it, and adjust it to fit the swelling and support it until it is healed and the collarbone (furcula) fracture has coagulated.
And it is expected to be strong and stable in 28 days, but in some cases it may take less time depending on the individual.
Chapter 6.
Chapter 6: On the healing of a shoulder fracture.
A spatula-shaped bone (likely the scapula or shoulder blade) is broken across its width and not through it, but rather at the edges.
And so when it is broken at the site, and it is broken in the middle of the bone, and it is not known except by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture, prepare for it to be reduced following its natural form in every way that is possible for you to do so.
Then place over the site a powder of millet flour with albumen (white) of egg and lion's fat; and also place a double amount of powdered cloth; and also place over it a thin board the size of the entire scapula, or slightly larger.
But if the board is concave in some areas of the scapula, then fill those concavities with lion's fat, and also raise the board so that it is level with the surface of the bone.
Then strengthen it from above with a long bandage, wrapped tightly but not too tight, and also ensure the board remains in place without moving.
And observe the binding every day, and whenever it becomes soft, bind it again firmly, adjusting and maintaining the board in its proper position if it has moved from its place.
And also have the patient lie on their side, with the affected limb supported by a sling.
And the scapula should be healed in 20 or 25 days.
And when this number of days has passed, then you can loosen the binding, knowing that there is no danger of damaging or disturbing the healing of the bone.
And if pus develops in the site of the bone and it spreads to the skin, then you need to make an incision over it, remove it, and proceed as described earlier for treating a collarbone (furcula) fracture or gout-related abscess.
Chapter 7:
Chapter 7: Healing of foot fracture.
The foot is often broken in the middle of the bone, and this type of fracture can be difficult to treat. However, the ends of the bones are more likely to become crushed or comminuted.
When the middle of the foot is broken, it often tilts towards the inner side, causing great pain, difficulty in walking and breathing, dizziness, and vomiting blood. The break is visible and cannot be missed as it creates a cavity in the bone that can be felt.
The healing of a broken foot involves setting the bone back in its place by having the patient lie on their back with a cushion placed between their shoulders. The muscles are then pressed and adjusted to align the ribs with the hands, while carefully operating and adjusting the bone until it regains its original shape.
Then place a plaster and padding over the affected area and cover it with a thin board made of fir or cedar, or similar lightweight materials, which is then wrapped in cloth. Carefully align the bone without causing any harm, using bandages to maintain its position. Observe the binding at all times, and when it becomes soft, tighten it again firmly.
If the need arises for removal due to infection or inflammation, you should cut and loosen the binding, remove any plaster, and treat the problem with appropriate medical care. Once the issue has been resolved, reapply the plaster, adjusting it as necessary, and continue to maintain it until the bone is fully healed.

Chapter 8: Healing of Fractured Ribs
Know that rib fractures usually only occur in thick areas close to the back, and not in the front where they are made up of cartilage. Therefore, any trauma to the front of the ribs is unlikely to cause a fracture. The knowledge of a rib fracture can be confirmed by examining it with touch, as the pain and tenderness are easily noticeable during palpation.
The healing of a rib fracture involves realigning the break with gentle manipulation and supporting it with a plaster cast or splint as necessary. This will help to hold the bones in place while they heal naturally.
If the rib fracture is tilting towards the inside, then it can cause intense pain and puncturing sensations, similar to those experienced by someone with pleurisy. This happens because the broken bone presses against the lining of the lungs, leading to difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood. Such a fracture is difficult to heal.
And the ancients have devised various ingenious methods for treating such conditions.
And one of their teachings is that the patient's food should promote inflation and flatulence in order to expand the abdomen, which will help push the fractured ribs back into place.
However, we have found that this method can sometimes cause pus-filled abscesses if not performed correctly, so we advise against it unless absolutely necessary. If an abscess has already formed, then it should be treated accordingly.
Some of the ancients recommended applying cupping glasses (ventoza) on the skin and creating suction to help realign the ribs. This is similar to reasoning, but there is a risk that the weakened state could cause the superfluidities to be drawn towards the site of injury, leading to complications.
Some of the ancients recommended covering the affected area with wool soaked in cauterizing oil, and placing powder between the ribs until it is full. The bandage should be wrapped around the patient's chest following the contour of their body. This method of treatment is similar to that used for pleurisy, combining dietary and medicinal care.
If the constriction causes extreme discomfort for the patient and one of the broken ribs is piercing the lung lining with a sharp pain, then there is concern for the patient's safety. In this case, it is necessary to make an incision on the site, expose the fractured rib, place a suitable instrument (such as a rib spreader) over it, and carefully extract the broken piece. This procedure requires great skill and precision.
Then we should adjust the edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitches, and treat it with plasters until it heals.
If an internal abscess occurs, then you should apply poultices made of roasted oil and place them over the affected area. The patient should also be treated for the internal abscess as necessary.
And have the patient lie on their side with the fracture raised above the level of the heart until it heals completely.

Chapter 9: Healing of Fractures in the Vertebrae of the Back and Neck.
Sometimes, there are also fractures in the bones of the neck or the vertebrae of the spine, which can be similarly problematic. (Note: No text provided for this topic) When such a fracture occurs and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider the following: If you see that the person's muscles are weakened, numb, and unresponsive, and when you press on them or prick them with a needle, they do not feel pain, then you should know that it is unlikely to heal. In such cases, the prognosis is often poor.
However, if the patient can feel pain when you touch or press on the affected area and when you prick it with a needle, then you can be assured that the nerve in the bone is still intact, and with proper treatment, the patient will recover.
If you observe similar symptoms in the vertebrae of the neck and want to determine if it will heal or not, then consider the patient's legs.
Now, if you see that the legs are already weak and similar symptoms as in the muscles have occurred, and when they lie on their back, there is bloating and involuntary bowel movements, and sometimes they are turned over to urinate but cannot do so, then you should know that it is fatal, and therefore, you should not waste your efforts in treating them.
But if none of those symptoms occur, the prognosis is better and less severe.
The treatment for this condition involves using heat to reduce the swelling in the affected vertebrae. This can be done by applying a warm compress, such as a cloth soaked in roasted oil or heated mustard seeds, directly onto the affected area several times a day until the swelling subsides. Additionally, the patient should avoid any movements that may exacerbate the pain and inflammation, such as turning their neck or lifting heavy objects. [.ix.]
And then apply a strengthening and comforting plaster, securing it with bandages. Command the patient to rest and remain calm, avoiding sleeping on the affected side until fully healed.
If there is separation in the bone of the skull or any other cause, then it is necessary to cut open the scalp and remove the separated piece of bone. After that, align the edges of the wound with stitches if it is large, and cure it with a suitable ointment until it heals.
If the back of the skull is broken and it's the end of the spine, then you need to insert your left index finger into the anus and realign the fractured bone with your other hand as best you can. Then apply a plaster and board if necessary, securing them in place.
If you feel that the skull bones are fractured, then you should trepan (make a hole) in them to remove the broken pieces and treat the wound with the appropriate surgical procedure until it heals.

Chapter 10: Healing of a Fractured Jaw
The bones of the wide hips are often broken, and if they break, then the fracture is not only at the ends of those bones but also extends in length and tends to bend towards the inner parts; and it happens to the patient pain in the area and puncture, and the leg becomes numb when there is a fracture.
And the healing of it is for you to place your hand on top of the affected area over the fracture, which is the shape of it (the injury).
And if the fracture is only in the limb, then you bandage that one according to what is appropriate for you to use, with a splint that aligns with its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the thigh or has already begun to bend towards the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on their stomach so that you can prepare for the healing of that fracture. And so once you have bandaged it (the injury), place a plaster over it and then place a wooden splint or stick over that, and tie it firmly with a bandage, making sure not to fear puncture from the fracture or dislocation of the splint.
And bandage the cavity with what fills that space so that it takes on an even compression, and tell the patient to sleep on their back or side.
But if an abscess forms, then you cease any attempts at extension or healing of the injury until the abscess has been treated, and then place over it what is prescribed for that; and then return to the healing of the injury, tying it firmly as necessary.
And if the bone becomes necrotic or is shattered, then you treat it.
Chapter 11.
Chapter 11: On the healing of a dislocated shoulder.
The term "adjutori" refers to the area between the upper arm and the head of the shoulder joint.
And if it is dislocated, then the healing of it can be achieved in one of two ways, the first of which is for you to take a stick of medium thickness and shape it as follows:
And there should be two ligaments at both ends, and it should be suspended from an elevated location.
