Chapter 13.
Chapter 13: On the healing of fractures in the thumb and fingers.
Know that the thumb of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers are prone to experiencing frequent abrasions but not necessarily fractures.
And when there is a fracture or sprain in the hand, then it is appropriate for the patient to keep their hand elevated and facing forward, and place their hand on top of an extended surface.
And let the assistant straighten the broken bones and let the doctor bandage those inside so that they are aligned with good alignment.
And then it is appropriate to apply a plaster and gauze, unless there is an abscess present. In this case, place a layer over the top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in linen cloth.
But if the bandage is on the inner parts near the palm of the hand, then you should make a pouch from cloth and instruct the patient to clench their fist inside it, and let it be held together with long strips of cloth.
And let the layer be made of leather with fabric on it, so that the leather is applied to the palm, and secure it as necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is appropriate to place layers on top and another layer below on the palm side, so that the hand remains open and straight; and then apply bandages as if turning the hand at the wrist with gentle movements.
And if you encounter a fracture in one of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as appropriate and held with the palm; and if necessary, place a small straight splint on it to elevate the fracture and prevent deformity.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger or index finger, or in the ring finger or pinky, then it should be bandaged with that finger alone, so that each finger is wrapped individually; or it is better to wrap all of them together continuously. Or place a small splint on top of it, just as we said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment and restoration, the disposition of abscesses or other issues, and address them with appropriate procedures according to their specific characteristics, when any cause arises, as we have mentioned before.
Chapter 14.
Chapter 14: On the healing of fractures in the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken, and this can be detected by sensation, as it feels different on the front and upper parts.
And the healing of it is done by strengthening the ligaments above and below the fracture, with the patient lying on their stomach and face down.
And let the assistant extend all the ligaments to their respective ends according to quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the knee joint of the thigh bone, then let the ligament be pulled up to the kneecap at the end of the patella, so that the extension pulls upward, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the ankle, then the ligament should be attached to the ankle, and the extension should be towards the lower parts.
Then the doctor should pull the bone with both hands to reduce it to its normal shape and join it properly; after that, apply a dressing and bandages, unless there is a risk of infection in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess subsides and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done in the following way: wrap a firm bandage over the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess material; then wrap the thigh, making sure to reach the calf and ankle.
And insert a long piece of string or thread between the calf and thigh near the ankle towards the lower parts, and make sure that the ends of the string are above the highest point of both parts; then wind it around the leg and calf so that any excess bandage material is removed.
Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture and attach one splint to the bone of the calf.
Then fill any gaps between the thigh and calf with soft cloths to ensure that the bandaging is secure.
Then begin the firm bandaging starting from the middle of the fracture with three or four layers, and as you move away from the site of the fracture, make the bandage lighter, softer, and more loose.
Then stretch the two ends of the string or thread to the lower parts until it reaches the heel of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side to ensure that the bandage does not come loose.
Then leave the bandage in place as long as there is no risk of tissue damage, infection, or swelling, and if any of these conditions occur, then loosen the bandage and correct the problem immediately, as we have mentioned many times before.
But if pus forms in the bone and comes to the surface, then it is necessary to lance it if possible; and if not, leave it alone and treat it with a suitable remedy until it heals.
And sometimes it is necessary to use splints to stabilize this thigh fracture, just as we mentioned for the forearm and arm injuries.
However, if the patient develops a limp after such treatment, then it is necessary to apply a splint to the other leg as well.
And you should know that the thigh typically takes 40 days or a little more or less to heal, depending on various factors and individual circumstances.
Chapter 15.
On the treatment of ankle fractures.
You should know that ankle fractures take a long time to heal, but they often cause significant swelling.
But if the ankle is only bruised or has a minor fracture with one or two cracks in the bones, then it can be treated with rest and immobilization.
The treatment of a fractured ankle involves reducing and realigning the broken bones, then immobilizing them in their proper position using appropriate methods based on ease of application and availability.
Then apply a dressing and place a round splint over it if necessary; and secure it with an appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the precautions that we have recommended for other types of fractures, including infection and other complications, taking appropriate measures until the injury has healed.
Chapter 16.
On the treatment of a leg fracture.
You should know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the calf bone (fibula), and the other is thin and is called the shin bone (tibia).
Fractures of the leg bones can occur in various forms, similar to those that affect the forearm bones, and treatment involves reducing and realigning both bones in a manner similar to that used for arm fractures, with a single surgical procedure.
If both leg bones are broken together, the entire leg is considered to be fractured.
If the smaller bone (tibia) is broken, then the leg is considered to be fractured in the front.
If the larger bone (fibula) is broken, then it extends below the smaller bone and is one of the more severe types of leg fractures.
Then apply extension and alignment techniques, as well as fixation of the splints, according to the specific treatment method being used.
However, if the leg fracture is old and has already developed significant stiffness or contractures, then extension should be performed gently and gradually, with careful attention to alleviating any discomfort or pain caused by the procedure.
And in the case of a leg operation that is similar to an arm procedure, such as when splints are applied to stabilize the fracture and complete the entire treatment process, you should use wooden pegs placed below the knee joint, which are made from the branches of palm trees or other materials with small diameters, but not from thicker materials or those that could cause injury. The length of these pegs should correspond to the length of the leg from the ankle to just below the knee.
Then wrap each bandage around the leg in two layers, covering the entire length of the leg from the ankle to just below the knee on both sides.
Then secure the splints in three places at both ends and in the middle to prevent the leg from moving or rotating sideways, ensuring that it remains in proper alignment.
Then apply a wooden splint of appropriate length to the leg and secure it in place to maintain stability and prevent movement.
It is especially important to follow these steps in cases of fractures, particularly when there is a clean break or crack in the bone.
Then monitor the leg daily and study it carefully to detect any signs of infection or inflammation, taking appropriate measures if necessary until the injury has healed completely.
Chapter 17.
On the treatment of ankle fractures, foot bones fractures, and toe fractures.
Ankle fractures are relatively rare, but foot bone fractures can occur in various forms. Toe fractures, on the other hand, are less common and typically involve only bruising or minor injuries.
If a foot bone fracture occurs and the bones are displaced or elevated, then have the patient walk on the injured foot with proper support to promote healing and alignment.
Then place the foot on an elevated surface to reduce swelling and promote healing of the displaced bones. Apply gentle pressure and massage to help realign the bones in their proper position.
Then apply a dressing and secure it in place with bandages. Place a small board or plate under the arch of the foot, supported by two pads, to provide additional support and stability.
Then secure the foot in a comfortable, stable position using appropriate bandages or other materials to hold it in place and prevent further injury.
After three or four days, carefully remove the bandages and assess the injury. If you find that the bones are aligned or displaced, then use appropriate techniques to realign them as needed.
If any toes are broken or fractured, then realign and treat them as necessary, using similar techniques to those described for finger injuries in the hand. Then apply a splint to the broken toe that is slightly longer than the toe itself and positioned at an angle for proper alignment. Place a small board or plate under the arch of the foot for additional support and stability, then secure everything in place with appropriate bandages or other materials.
If the broken bone is between two, or three, or more, then place a stake on each knuckle of the corresponding size wrapped in a lion's cloth, and raise the table at the foot of the bed, and have one of the table legs be under the belly of the foot to provide good support.
And it is appropriate for you to remember that not all the causes that have been mentioned in other fractures and accidents should be considered, and deal with each accident as necessary.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone (femur) and the point of the hipbone (os pubis) and the rod (virga) of a man (penis).
When the thigh bone (femur) is broken, then make an incision on the rounded side of the hip, and slightly incline it towards the buttock, and have it held in place from behind.
And let the midwife fill the thigh bone (femur) with cotton, and do the same in the thigh of her as one would expect; and let the woman be shaken, and slowly lift her legs little by little, for all of that must come out before the fractured end of the thigh bone is in front of the buttock and the broken bone returns to its place.
And place a pillow under her back, and when she wants to urinate, soften the cotton with ease and let her urinate, and return it as one would at the beginning, and then return her to her original pillow.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for that is how long it takes to heal.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and place a cane reed cannula on its tip; then insert the entire bladder into the thigh bone (femur) of the patient and inflate it through the cannula with strength until the bladder is inflated inside the thigh bone, for the fracture will return to its original position.
And let it be filled with cotton and remain in place for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the hipbone (os pubis) of a man or woman is broken, then administer in healing and joining it what we have recommended for the thigh bone (femur).
And what is right is not hidden in these similar fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who has some study, practice and understanding of this book; for a fracture indicates to you in major cases the right path in healing and joining it, so understand well.
But when the penis (virga) of a man is broken, then take a guarguamela (type of vegetable) of a duck, and put the penis in it; and place an wrapping of cloth over it, and bandage it, and let it be left alone for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
Of the healing of bone fractures when they are infected.
It is appropriate for one who has a fracture with infection, especially if the bone is large such as the thigh bone (femur) or the shinbone (tibia), or similar to that, to be careful and perform bloodletting immediately if the conditions of bloodletting indicate so as we have said before. Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay, or else pus will form.
But if pus forms, then you should postpone the restoration of the fracture until the ninth day, until the pus has subsided, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, because you will cause further harm.[](https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.gmail.heagoo.romance&gl=US&pli=1)

And it should be round and slightly curved, so that it does not bend during compression on the bone at the time of operation.

This is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labbrent voluntarium", which means a small palette.
Therefore, the sharp end must be raised above the protruding bone's edge.

And then smooth the roughness and small scratches in the bone.

When you have completed the restoration of the bone, then submerge a cloth in wine that has been steeped with styptic herbs, especially during summer, and do not place any substance on the wound that contains oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in the wound.
And then administer styptic herbs during the completion of the bone restoration, and leave the wound open so that you can insert a probe with forceps, and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be cautious not to irritate the wound with the fracture, as many foolish doctors have done this and caused harm or death to their patients, causing suppuration or corruption, which is harmful. And if the tissue is slightly soft and different from the tissue of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or severe, and you fear the complications that we have mentioned, and there is pain in the area, then it is not advisable to apply styptic herbs; instead, use hard bandages for the dressing and strengthen them with those.
And when after one or two days the wound begins to develop pus, then remove the cloth that you had placed over it with wine and apply hot compresses, using ointments that are commonly used for dressing wounds, such as tetrafarmacon or similar, according to our custom.
And it is your responsibility to loosen the bandages and examine the wound every day in the morning and evening until it has healed and solidified, and ensure that the limb is positioned in such a way that pus can easily drain from it towards the lower parts.
If the wound takes many days to heal and is not solidified or separated from the pus, then know that this is a sign of putrefaction, and it is necessary to examine the wound thoroughly with probes. And of these putrefactions, those that are separated and extruded should be left alone, but those that are not separated and cause pressure on the limb, causing pain, you should try to cut into them if possible.
But if there is necrosis or corruption, such as stenomas or other forms of putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply one of the following remedies according to what is appropriate for the treatment, which has been described in its chapter.
And in this matter, let my words be clear and you attend to your own actions: when the bone is large and shattered, it protrudes from the limb like the bones of the thigh or the shoulder and similar bones of larger members, and do not attempt to pull it out or extract it, because many have died as a result; instead, leave it in place for as long as possible, because it will likely fall away on its own after 20 or 30 days. And then treat the wound if it is visible to you, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20
On the treatment of knots that occur in the aftermath of a fracture wound.
Often, this knot occurs in the aftermath of a fracture wound, especially near joints, and it takes on the shape of the limb, causing obstruction and hindering the limb's natural movement.
Therefore, consider if the knot is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, gum arabic, myrrh, dragon's blood, and similar substances; take some or all of these and mix them with wine that has been steeped in styptic herbs, or albumen from egg whites, or vinegar. Apply this mixture to the knot on a piece of cloth and bind it tightly over the affected area. [
Keep the bandage in place for several days without loosening it, then remove it and apply a new one until the knot has receded; or strengthen it with a sheet of lead, as lead has the property of healing all inflammation in limbs.
But if the knot has turned into a hard stone and is tight, and there is a need to remove it carefully, then cut it along its highest part, remove the excess and protruding parts, or smooth it with sharp instruments until it has receded; and treat the wound inside until it heals.
Chapter 21
On the restoration of fractures when, even after healing, the limb remains weak and fragile, not as it was before.
When a fracture has healed but the limb remains weak and fragile, this is due to several causes: one of them being excessive removal of bandages or inadequate binding; another is tightness in the ligaments preventing nutrition from reaching the limb; yet another cause is excess fluid accumulation; or movement at an improper time; or lack of blood in the patient's body and overall weakness. [
And the treatment for this is to feed the patient well and stimulate their appetite so that their blood becomes abundant; give them baths; and engage in activities that bring joy and happiness to them.
Therefore, apply a poultice to the limb to attract nutrients to it, or continuous application of compresses with warm water until nutrition returns to its natural shape and form.[
Chapter 22 [
Chapter 22: On the treatment of shattered bones when they have healed in a twisted manner and hinder their proper function.
When a member that has already healed develops twistedness, lumpiness, or knotting in the restored bone, causing it to appear distorted but not impeding its natural movement, one should not assume that the member is broken again based on appearances alone. Instead, careful examination and testing of the limb's function should be conducted before making any conclusions.
However, there have been many foolish doctors and bone setters who have made this mistake in our land, which is highly detrimental and causes great fear due to the danger it poses. This operation should be avoided at all costs, as its reputation carries a risk of death.
However, if the lameness and knot are recent, then they should be treated with warm water compresses containing softening herbs such as elder flowers, radish, melilot, and similar substances. A plaster of softening agents can also be made with well-prepared diallage or by mashing althea root with linseed oil and applying it as a plaster. Alternatively, ripe figs can be mashed with dove dung and applied as a poultice, along with other medicines known for their soothing and consolidating properties.
And sometimes it is dissolved with gentle continuous friction that is done with moist hands, and movement of the affected part is administered in all directions at all times.
But if the condition has become chronic and hardened, turned into stone, and requires surgery to cure it, then it is necessary to cut out the pus above the affected area and dissolve the continuation of the bone, and remove any excess matter from the nodule with fine incisors. And this should be done with care and attention in a sterile environment.
Chapter 23.
On speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its proper location, which prevents movement and distorts the shape of the affected limb, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to act quickly to reduce it, and not delay the reduction, because if it is delayed, the area will become infected, making it difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to lie down or stand in the hour of infection, because often it brings on spasms and sharp pains.
But when this happens, it is necessary to proceed with the bloodletting of the patient, and have them lie down so that the infection can rest; and the affected limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and reduced gently.
And the entire limb should be treated with that which will bring healing in its time.
And I have already organized the chapters on dislocation as described in the section on fracture; from the uppermost part of the body to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Both jaws can become dislocated if not treated; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: either they are moved from their places by slight removals and are slightly softened, or they are dislocated entirely and completely, so that they become soft at the part of the joint, saliva flows from the patient's mouth, and he cannot hold it or close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in its word.
But when the dislocation is slight, it can be returned to its place with little cause in most cases.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then gentle reduction must be administered without delay.
And it is necessary that the caregiver hold the patient's head, and the doctor insert the tip of his thumb from one hand into the socket of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth of the patient if the dislocation is on one side; or he should insert his two thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of both hands should be outside holding the cheeks; and he should command the patient to cease moving their jaws and release it from either side; and he should guide the jaw back into place and push it inward until it returns to its location.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil to make the reduction easier and not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And so when they return and are guided back into place, and the patient's mouth is closed and not relaxed, then place cotton balls covered in cerote (a type of wax) over those areas, and make it with wax and oil rubbed; and then gently apply bandages made from soft linen.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head should be positioned between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to the right or left side; and do not force them to chew for any reason; once their teeth have been realigned and the jaw is no longer dislocated, then they can chew as they please.
And that should be administered easily, and you should not struggle with the opening of the mouth for food, drink, and expectoration, until the jaw becomes relaxed and healed.
And if it is difficult to realign the two jawbones when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their proper places, often fevers and constant swelling occur, and the patient's belly becomes bloated very quickly, and they vomit pure bile very easily.
And when you see this, you should know that it is perilous; and it often happens that the person who experiences this dies within 10 days.

Chapter 27.
Chapter 27: Treatment of Hip Dislocation.
You should know that the hip joint is difficult to dislocate and equally difficult to set back in place; it can become dislocated in any direction, but most commonly at the front or back. The dislocation will not be hidden from you, as it will be evident whether the leg is bent or twisted in a certain way depending on how it is dislocated. When you adjust the dislocated hip joint with your hand, it should be clear to you that the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or touch its own muscle.
It is necessary to act quickly and reduce the dislocation before it becomes infected with purulent matter (abscess), as this will make reduction difficult and may lead to incomplete healing. This is especially true for dislocations at the back, which are more severe than other types of dislocation and can be accompanied by significant pain and a high risk of mortality.
The restoration of the dislocated hip joint, when it is possible to reduce it, involves extending the patient's leg with two hands, spreading their arms apart, and placing the hands of the medical practitioner on the affected area while applying gentle pressure. This can be done either by pushing the joint back into place using the fingertips or the palm of the hand. The goal is to guide the joint back into its proper position safely and effectively.
But if the dislocation is at the front, then return it by bending the leg towards the patient's chest until the heel of the hand strikes the hip joint.
And if the dislocation does not respond to reduction, then administer strong and forceful extension.
And have two assistants hold the arms and two others maintain traction to prevent it from slipping back during extension.
And then rotate the arms in all directions, as they are wrapped with long folded cloth or bandages around the patient's hands.
And when the medical practitioner touches the joint with their hand, anoint it with some oil to facilitate its movement and ease of reduction. Then apply firm pressure until it returns to its proper position.
And for the reduction of it (the hip joint), a plaster with styptic and astringent properties made from albumen (egg white) should be applied, and the leg should be held in place with a bandage that is not too tight. The affected limb should be suspended from a sling around the neck, and left to rest for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and so if the joint has returned to its proper position, then you can release the ligament and leave it alone.
And if you see that the joint is not strengthening, then reapply the plaster and ligament, and leave it on for several days until it has healed; and then release it.
But if there is excessive weight or difficulty in reducing the joint and the patient's condition is unstable, then administer gentle hydrotherapy and mild friction massage with compression until symptoms are relieved; or apply a compress of warm fat to the affected area, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and night. And then apply that (the compress), and place the patient in a bath; when it becomes dry, gently rub the affected joint with tempered friction, and reapply the warm compress three times, allowing it to rest on the joint for a brief period between each application. Corruption has already spread in the inner part of the nail, before it has been trimmed.
And if desired, place the affected area over a warm and moist cow's wound with a bandage and strong pressure, doing this several times, as it is moist and helps restore its normal condition.

Chapter 28: Treatment of Dislocation of the Wrist Joint
"The wrist is dislocated sometimes, and reducing the displacement of it is easy, but the contrary is true for other joints. However, it is crucial to act quickly in reducing it in the hour it becomes dislocated, before the location becomes inflamed or infected with pus."
"And reducing the dislocation is done by placing the patient's joint on a table and having them extend their arm. Then, place your hand or their palm over the affected area and push gently until it returns to its proper location."
"However, one must consider if the palm is facing the stomach of the arm, then have the patient place the back of their arm on a table for extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is on the back of the arm, then position the patient's arm flat on the table, allowing the doctor's hand to be placed over the affected area."

"However, if the joint remains dislocated due to its twisted state, and the patient feels no cause for it, but their limb is weakened and unable to be strengthened, then know that the nerves are compressed or trapped. In this case, do not force the reduction, but instead apply a heated cautery to release the compression and gently attempt reduction again."
"Once the dislocation has been reduced, place an herbal plaster over it as recommended and leave it on for 5 days to heal. Afterwards, remove the plaster and begin exercising the limb again."
"But if movement is hindered and there is serious swelling, then apply warm water with herbs and gently massage the area until it feels better."

Chapter 21.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle only involve dislocation, and do not involve what happens with other joints of small displacement and concavity.
And the hip joint can be dislocated in four ways: it can be dislocated inwardly, outwardly, forward, or backward.
And most commonly, it is dislocated inwardly, and rarely forward or backward.
And the sign of a dislocation inwardly is that when you place the healthy leg next to the affected leg, the latter appears longer and more prominent than the former, and the patient cannot bend their knee without pain, which indicates that the swelling caused by an abscess has reached the area where the thigh joins the hip.
And the sign of someone who experiences a dislocation outwardly is that their symptoms are the opposite of those described above.
And the sign of someone who experiences a dislocation forward is that they extend their leg fully but are unable to bend it due to pain, and if they try to walk, they cannot do so because of discomfort in the front part of their hip; their urine may be retained and there may be swelling in the affected area, and they should be made to walk on their toes.
And the sign of someone who experiences a dislocation backward is that they cannot extend their knee or bend it without also bending their thigh, and their leg appears shorter than the other, with a softened and swollen area at the site where the hip meets the thigh.
But the reduction of dislocations depends on whether it is old or new. If it is an old and chronic dislocation, then you should apply a reduction treatment based on its specific condition. However, if it is not possible to reduce it, then you should avoid applying any cure-all medicine.
But in the case of someone with a recent dislocation, which is one of the four types, you should consider returning the joint to its normal position by extending it outwardly and inwardly, then moving it to the right and left, because it will easily return to its normal position without requiring any further treatment.
But if it does not return to its normal position in this way, then you should prepare the following equipment: extend the patient's leg downward or upward using a bandage tied around their thigh or knee; have another person place their hand on top of the affected area and press down gently on the tendons below the kneecap; apply strong pressure with a bandage tied tightly around the hip joint, and have another person hold the free end of the bandage firmly.
And this extension involves stretching the affected area toward the shinbone or the back part of the hip toward the spine.
And this extension involves lifting the patient's entire body off the ground and suspending them in the air using their own strength.
And this type of extension is common to all four types of dislocations.
And so if the dislocation returns with the method we described, that will be good. But if not, then you should consider specific treatments for each type of dislocation.
In reducing a dislocation inwardly, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their side and place a bandage over the hip joint at the point where the thigh meets the hip, between the top of the thighbone and the area below the kneecap. Then, apply strong pressure with a bandage tied around the upper part of the shinbone, starting from the ankle and moving upwards towards the knee.
And have another person strongly grip the patient's arms and pull them outward while applying strong pressure to the affected side of the hip, extending it forcefully, because this will cause the dislocation to return to its normal position.
And this type of reduction is easier than the others for reducing this limb.
But if the patient resists and does not comply with this type of treatment, then you should tie both their legs together firmly using a bandage around their ankles and calves.
And each of the patient's legs should be four finger-widths long, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two finger-widths.
And have the patient suspended from the top of a post, with their body facing upward, and at a height of two arm-lengths from the ground.
And command a strong young person to push down on the patient's hip while another person holds onto the patient and pushes their hip with great force, causing the joint to return to its normal position quickly.
But in reducing a dislocation outwardly, it is necessary for the patient to sit on a stool as we recommended after heating the area, and then apply strong pressure with a bandage tied around the affected thigh and ankle.
And have two posts placed on either side of the patient: one under their feet and the other at their head. Also place a post in the middle of the stool on which a heated cloth is wrapped, so that the patient does not suffer from discomfort caused by the post between their legs, and the extension of the leg downward is not hindered.
And have each person extend their respective area, and the doctor use their hand to guide the reduction according to medical art.
And when the dislocation is in front, then it is necessary to extend the leg slightly and place the patient on a stool as we described earlier. Then, place the palm of your right hand on the kneecap of the affected leg and use your left hand to press down firmly on the area above the ankle, causing it to move towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is at the back, then it is not necessary to extend the leg downward and the patient should be elevated off the ground. It is necessary for them to lie on a firm surface, as we recommended earlier when describing how to place the patient in a standing position if their hip is dislocated outwardly, according to what we said about supporting their leg with two wide boards. The legs should be extended and placed on a firm surface, as we described earlier.
And it is necessary to apply pressure using a board on the area where the joint protrudes.
And so when the reduction is complete, as necessary - and you will know that it is successful if you see that the patient's legs are extended equally and they can move their affected leg without difficulty, indicating that it has returned to its normal position - then bring the patient's knees together and apply a paste and bandage, ensuring that the hip joint is not damaged in any way. Have the patient rest for three or four days.
And then remove the bandage and paste, and compare one leg with the other.
And so if you see that the legs are equal in length and movement, then you can be sure that the dislocation has been successfully reduced, and you can allow the patient to walk again.
And if you notice any swelling or softness, then reapply the bandage and paste, and extend the leg again as before, allowing it to rest for three days.
And then remove the bandage and allow the patient to walk slowly after several days, until they are fully recovered.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The dislocation happens in three ways: it can occur in the outer parts, in the inner parts, and in the lower parts, that is, the back parts; but it does not occur in the front parts.
And the sign of a dislocation is that you should command the patient to bend their leg towards the thigh; if they are unable to do so, then you know that the joint is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocations is done by having the patient lie straight, so that they can extend their leg if possible; place your fingers on the affected joint and hold the patient's foot between yours; then apply pressure with both palms to reduce the dislocation while a helper extends the patient's limb back into its original position.
And the sign of a successful reduction is that the patient's leg will easily adhere to their thigh without pain or discomfort.
And then apply a dressing on it and have the patient keep their leg adhered to their thigh for three or four days; then remove the bandages.
And have the patient avoid walking for a few days until they are healed and comfortable.
But if reduction is not possible for you, then administer vigorous stretching and manipulation as described in the previous narrative on hip surgery.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of ankle dislocation.
Sometimes the ankle is sprained due to minor trauma, and sometimes it is dislocated in full or partially, which can happen either on the inner or outer side of the joint.
And the sign of a dislocation is that you see the toe swollen, proceeding in the direction of the part it is dislocated to.
But the treatment for reducing a dislocation is relatively easy, and that is to gently stretch it with your hands and cover it so that it returns (to its proper position).
But in the treatment of a dislocation, when it is dislocated according to its nature, you must make the patient sit up straight, and have a strong and capable servant stand behind them at their side, in the middle where they are dislocated.
And then you place your right hand on the upper part of the leg and your left hand on the lower part of the leg at the level of the ankle.
And then pull the leg towards you with your right hand and push it twice with your left hand, and stretch it out with your left hand; and press the heel of the foot with your right hand against the thigh without working too hard.
And do that two times as we have said.
And then press the heel of the foot against the thigh for the third time and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two times according to this method, and you see that the foot is swollen, that's good; but if not, then repeat the operation, because it will return (to its proper position).
But if you are prohibited from reducing it according to what we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground and place a strong board under them, which fits between their thighs, and you have already covered them with a blanket so that they do not get cold.
And have the servant of the patient hold their thighs and have another servant stretch the leg with their hands or use a lever to lift it; and have each servant pull against the extension on their side, and the board should be placed between the patient's thighs holding them apart, so that the patient's body does not move downwards with the extension.
And have the doctor cover the dislocation with their hands and another servant hold the healthy thigh downwards, because the dislocation will return easily.
And when the dislocation has returned and you have declared that the reduction is successful, then apply an emplaster and use a strong bandage to wrap it, and bind the leg to the thigh.
And you must be careful or cautious of the nerves which are located behind the ankle, because applying a bandage too forcefully over it may cause damage.
And leave that for two or three days; and if the bandage has softened, remove it.
And untie it on the third day or the fourth, and forbid the patient from moving for 40 days. But if they are forced to move before that time, it is not safe for the dislocation as it may become dissolved and corrupted, and furthermore, it will not receive treatment.
And if an abscess forms, then it is necessary to administer in the treatment of it what we have mentioned before in our narrative, in another location for treatment and incision, until it has matured.
Chapter 34.
Chapter 34: Treatment of dislocation of the toes.
It is necessary that the dislocated part be covered with a small amount of extension, but not too much, and this is not difficult to do, in fact it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in the joints of the ankle of the foot, then it is necessary to make the patient lie down with their leg extended on a level surface such as the ground or a table, and keep it in that position as if they were walking.
And then you stand with your weight on it, place your foot over the joints and press down with strength until it returns to its proper position and ensure that it is covered and not swollen in the affected area.
And then place a board under the bottom of their foot which supports the entire leg, with two ends, and hold it in place with firm and steady support for three days; and then untie it and let them rest from walking for several days until it is healed and recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Chapter 35: Types of dislocation caused by plague, or fracture, or any combination of them.
When any of those causes (plague or fracture) occur and you focus on the treatment and restoration of it, many times death will result. It is important to approach such cases with caution and humility, recognizing that there are some things beyond our control as medical practitioners. It is essential to do no harm, and sometimes that may mean referring the patient to a more experienced or specialized care provider, or simply providing comfort and support during their illness. It is also crucial to maintain good hygiene and sanitation practices in order to prevent the spread of infectious diseases like the plague.
And so it is not advisable to be overconfident in the treatment of such cases, but rather a wise and experienced practitioner who is merciful and compassionate, slow to act, not hasty or arrogant, should use gentle medicines at first that only reduce abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgement. But one should entrust oneself to the one in whom you hope for salvation from perdition, with alleviation of the illness, and in which there appears some hope. It is essential to have a humble attitude, recognizing the limits of human knowledge and expertise, and relying on divine guidance and grace in the healing process. The practitioner should also be cautious not to cause further harm or delay in seeking appropriate care for the patient, particularly in cases of serious illness or injury. And so make every effort to reduce the dislocation as soon as possible at the onset of the injury, before an abscess forms. This will increase the chances of successful treatment and reduce the risk of complications. The practitioner should be vigilant in monitoring the patient's progress and adjusting the treatment plan accordingly, seeking expert advice or consultation when necessary. It is important to maintain good communication with the patient and their family throughout the healing process, providing education and support as needed. Ultimately, the goal is to promote healing, alleviate suffering, and restore function to the affected area, with compassion and humility, recognizing that each patient is unique and deserving of individualized care and attention.
And so if the limb returns as we desire, then administer a regimen that reduces abscesses and treat the plague with drying agents that are appropriate for it. It is important to address any underlying infections or illnesses that may have contributed to the dislocation, using specific treatments and interventions based on the patient's individual needs and circumstances. The practitioner should be knowledgeable about various treatment options and able to make informed decisions about the most effective course of action for each case. This may involve consultation with other medical professionals or specialists, as well as ongoing monitoring and evaluation of the patient's progress. Ultimately, the goal is to promote healing, alleviate suffering, and restore function to the affected area, with compassion and humility, recognizing that each patient is unique and deserving of individualized care and attention.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and separate pieces of bone are involved, then you should align them first, and do what we have said in simple injuries that were mentioned before in their places. It is important to address any fractures or bone injuries as part of the treatment plan for a dislocation, using specific techniques and interventions based on the patient's individual needs and circumstances. The practitioner should be knowledgeable about various treatment options and able to make informed decisions about the most effective course of action for each case. This may involve consultation with other medical professionals or specialists, as well as ongoing monitoring and evaluation of the patient's progress. Ultimately, the goal is to promote healing, alleviate suffering, and restore function to the affected area, with compassion and humility, recognizing that each patient is unique and deserving of individualized care and attention.
And take care and avoid as much as you can any internal harm in the veins or arteries according to your ability, following my warning; because this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and truly beneficial for your body at the right time. The practitioner should be mindful of potential risks and complications associated with any medical intervention, and take all necessary precautions to minimize harm and promote healing. This includes following established guidelines and protocols for safe and effective treatment, as well as seeking ongoing education and training in order to stay current with best practices and emerging trends in the field. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, may God bless him. This passage concludes the section on dislocations in the medieval medical treatise attributed to the renowned Andalusian physician and surgeon Abu al-Qasim Khalaf ibn Abbas al-Zahrawi, also known as Albucasis. The text reflects the author's deep understanding of anatomy, physiology, and pathology, as well as his extensive clinical experience in diagnosing and treating various medical conditions. The passage emphasizes the importance of careful observation, accurate diagnosis, and individualized treatment plans, based on a thorough assessment of each patient's unique needs and circumstances. The practitioner is encouraged to approach each case with humility, compassion, and a commitment to excellence, recognizing the limits of human knowledge and expertise, and relying on divine guidance and grace in the healing process.
